==PAGE:1==
Foundation design methods for one-family
houses
A study of foundation design methods to increase the viability of introducing
innovative solutions
Master Thesis
DTU Civil Engineering
Department of Civil Engineering
Submitted by
Supervisors
Mads Dalsgaard Grunnan
Kristoffer Negendahl
DTU s220504
Yuepeng Dong
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
This thesis delves into the foundation design method in the Danish geotechni-
cal industry. The focus is on one-family houses and the imperative to reduce
the carbon footprint of their foundations. The research is anchored in a case
study called: Tversted House. By introducing the use of recycled glass material
and timber beams, it solves the foundation problem in an innovative way and,
conversely, inspired the research question:
”How can the existing foundation design method be changed to better implement
innovative perspectives?”
The methodological framework of the thesis evaluates the existing design method,
expands this method, and proposes a new design method. This proposed design
method integrates a holistic view of the foundation design, considering load,
structural, and soil parameters. A qualitative analysis of design considerations
is conducted on the basis of the application of both design methods to Tver-
sted House. The two designs are ultimately evaluated on a set of performance
criteria. The findings suggest that, while introducing additional considerations
to the existing design method increases flexibility, it presents significant chal-
lenges. Ultimately, three main improvements to the existing design method were
identified: first, focus on sustainable materials through regulations or design
guidelines; second, improve collaboration between project stakeholders to allow
for a more flexible design; and third, reduce material use by adjusting safety
factors based on detailed analysis of soil properties and construction materials.

neering, Technical University of Denmark, DTU. It has been done in the field of

==PAGE:4==
Reading guide
This guide will give a brief introduction to each chapter of this thesis and the
purpose they serve.
Chapter 1 Introduction, chapter 2 Overall Methodology,
chapter 3 Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review, chapter 4 Introduc-
tion of Design Methods, chapter 5 Analysis of Calculation Methods, chapter 6
Application of Design Methods, chapter 7 Evaluation of Design Methods, chapter
8 Discussion and chapter 9 Conclusion.
Chapter 1 explains how the idea for the project started, which questions were
raised, and outlines the general research question investigated.
The chapter
introduces the case study that served as an inspiration and is later used for the
application of the design methods.
Chapter 2 outlines the overall methodology that the thesis imposes and intro-
duces each step in the process that led to answering the research question. From
the introduction of the design methods to the application of the design methods,
to their evaluation and in the end to the discussion of the findings.
Chapter 3 introduces the basis of information for completing a foundation design
for a one-family house in Denmark. It focuses on three primary areas. The soil
and its properties, the soil bearing capacity formulas, and the different founda-
tion solutions found most commonly in the Danish context.
Chapter 4 serves to outline the existing design method and further expand this to
introduce a proposed design method. By outlining the existing design method
and defining a proposed design method, they can be compared against each
other.
Chapter 5 delves deeper into the specific calculation methods deployed in the ap-
plication of the design methods investigating which parameters have the largest
influence on the load-bearing capacity formulas for the soil.
Chapter 6. The previously introduced design methods are applied in this chapter
to the case study to base their results in practice.
Chapter 7 Based on the application of both design methods, the evaluation
is done based on an estimate of cost in terms of economic and environmental
impact. In addition, both design methods are evaluated on a set of performance
criteria to ensure that they are also assessed on qualitative parameters.
iii
==PAGE:5==
Chapter 8. Limitations that have influenced the application of the design meth-
ods are discussed. In addition, the discussion weighs both the proposed design
method and its limitations and the overall methodology of the thesis. Further-
more, the discussion includes a section on future work, based on the findings in
this project.
Chapter 9. Finally, the conclusion sums up the most important points through-
out the text and concludes the project.

==PAGE:10==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation and Problem Statement
In recent years, the construction industry has begun to evaluate its processes to
transition to greener practices. This includes the introduction of more wood in
structures(Byggeri, 2021), as well as solutions that could make concrete build-
ings easier to recycle (Bruun, 2022). Meanwhile, the requirements for energy
conservation in buildings are increasing (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2018). All
of this points to a reduction in the energy consumption of buildings over their
lifetime. These areas are evolving due to this growing interest, but what about
the foundations of the structures? It is estimated that up to 40% of the total
CO2 emissions from a one-family house are attributed to the foundation (Minter,
2014). This raises the question: How can we decrease the carbon dioxide footprint
of the foundation?
Drawing inspiration from other areas of engineering might be one way to solve the
problem. It would be natural to look at innovative solutions in other construction
areas and draw inspiration from these. The solution could be to substitute the
current materials as when structural components are swapped for timber instead
of concrete or steel. Could other materials be used for foundations?
Geotechnical engineering is a relatively new category of engineering, and working
with soil has often proved challenging, each situation being unique. This ends
up creating design methods that are partly based on estimations, and the end
result is often not a precise design.
Especially when it comes to one-family
houses (Pedersen, 2011). Consequently, the safety factors are high compared
to other areas of engineering (Standard, 2007d) (Standard, 2007a). Can these
uncertainties be eliminated by obtaining additional information on the soil?
The existing design method for the foundations of one-family houses is grounded
1
==PAGE:11==
Chapter 1. Introduction
in the Geotechnical Categories (Pedersen, 2011), which introduce both effective-
ness and security but also represent a more rigid approach to design and does
limit the actual foundation design (Jens Jacob Porsmose, 2024). This calls for a
change in the existing design method.
To be able to change or challenge the existing design method, one must outline
it before embarking on the process of reinventing and constructing an improved
one. Is it possible to change the existing design method and reinvent it? How
can it be done? This led to the following research question:
How can the existing foundation design method be changed to better implement
innovative perspectives?
A successfully proposed design method could challenge the existing design method
and impact the general approach to foundation design in Denmark by introducing
additional considerations in the design phase and adding additional parameters
to evaluate the success of the design.
1.2
Case
To apply the existing and proposed design methods and compare them with
each other, a case study is introduced. This makes it possible to conduct a di-
rect evaluation of the results and can help to inform where the strengths and
weaknesses of the two methods lie. The case study Tversted House is a one-
family house built in Northern Jutland. The case is of interest because it uses
cellglas and timber for the foundation. These two materials combine to form a
strip foundation. Furthermore, the theory of compensated foundation is used to
reduce settlements. The house as a whole is seen as a showcase of what different
biobased materials can be used for and how good they actually are as construc-
tion materials. This structure is at the moment one of a kind in Denmark. This
project, made possible through the dedicated efforts of Nikolova/Aarsø adopted
an uncompromising approach toward the use of sustainable materials. Anchor-
ing the thesis in a real-life project enhances the evaluation’s ability to address
the aforementioned questions more effectively. Additionally, the case serves as
an inspiration to investigate the chosen problem. It is especially interesting be-
cause it approaches the design of foundations for a one-family house in a radical
different way.
2
==PAGE:12==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.1: Tversted House
1.3
Approach
What started as a pure focus on the foundation of Tversted House turned into
a broader analysis of the existing design method currently used by the geotech-
nical community in Denmark. This existing method is outlined and compared
with a proposed design method. The comparison is made based on the design
solution that each design method proposes for the chosen case. The evaluation is
performed by comparing key factors and performing a qualitative analysis of the
different design methods. The end result of the project is a discussion on how to
change the current design approach based on the information gained throughout
the thesis. To limit the field of study, the thesis will only look at the foundations
of one-family house, as well as limit itself to typical Danish soil conditions. This
increases the viability of the design. The overall method of the thesis will be
introduced in the next chapter.
3
==PAGE:13==
Chapter 2. Overall Methodology
2
Overall Methodology
This chapter will outline the overall method employed in this thesis that will
be used to answer the research question. It describes the structure of the thesis
including how the project goes from identifying the design methods to applying
them, and, in the end, evaluating them.
2.1
Introduction of Design Methods
As part of the existing design method, the engineer defines the design based on
three Geotechnical Categories (Pedersen, 2011) (Standard, 2007d). The Geotech-
nical Categories allow the design to disregard considerations based on the cat-
egory, which can be both an advantage or a limitation. These considerations
and their influence are described in Section 4.2.1. Based on the literature (Ped-
ersen, 2011) and interviews with practitioners, the existing design method is
outlined (Vestergaard, 2023), (Jens Jacob Porsmose, 2024). This method will be
elaborated on further in Section 4.2.
The proposed design method is based on the existing design method, but intro-
duces additional design parameters, focusing on critical aspects of the foundation
design (Shaopei Lin, 2015). The approach views the foundation design in a more
integrated way and introduces a split of the design into three sections, load,
structural, and soil. A closer definition of what is present in each category will
be explained in Section 4.3. It argues that load and structural considerations are
necessary to create a balanced method when approaching the foundation design.
The introduction of these additional parameters makes it possible to adjust the
design based on additional information and can introduce a way to optimize the
foundation design.
The bearing capacity calculations introduced in Chapter 3 used in the Geotech-
nical Categories will be analyzed based on their parameters through sensitivity
4
==PAGE:14==
Chapter 2. Overall Methodology
analysis. It explores how varying key parameters affect the outcome of these cal-
culations (Saltelli et al., 2004). This analysis can help identify which parameters
have the greatest influence on design, especially under different conditions.
2.2
Application of Design Methods
Apart from serving as inspiration for the problem, Tversted House will be used
as the basis for a foundation design. When design methods are applied, they are
more easily comparable and provide insight into the strengths and weaknesses
of the methods, placing the investigation in a specific practical context. The
application can be found in Chapter 6 and focuses on highlighting the design
methods as a whole. The designs are limited by the information gained on the
project, and since the nature of the application is theoretical, the execution of
the design will not be evaluated. Furthermore, the design methods do not take
into account the terrain deck, and the application does not introduce a final
design.
2.3
Evaluation of Design Methods
By listing performance criteria and evaluating them based on a scoring system,
important points can be highlighted (Guillaume Marques, 2010). These crite-
ria include various factors such as flexibility, ease of application, environmental
impact, and economic efficiency.
This assessment helps to draw comparisons
between the different design methods. When evaluating on the basis of these
criteria, less measurable areas can be evaluated.
2.4
Partial Conclusion
The thesis reviews the current literature and engages key stakeholders in the in-
dustry to describe the existing foundation design method. Then a proposed de-
sign method is introduced that offers engineers greater freedom in design choices.
The design methods are then based on the design obtained when applied to the
case evaluated on a set of performance criteria. This ensures that the evaluation
takes into account a wider range of design considerations.
5
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
3
Foundation Design Principles -
Literature Review
Soil does unlike structural materials, which have well-defined properties(Jensen,
2016), presents a more variable and challenging medium.
This chapter aims
to provide essential information to understand how foundations are designed.
An overview of existing theories and solutions will be presented. This includes
an introduction to the most common soil types found in Denmark, including
considerations about the soil properties in Section 3.1 and how they are found
in Section 3.1.1. The commonly used foundation types are introduced in Section
3.2 with their bearing capacity formulas elaborated in Sections 3.3 and 3.4. No
further introduction to the design of structural members and load estimation is
given.
3.1
Soil Categorization
The design of structures subjected to soil stresses requires a understanding of
the soil and its various characteristics. The focus will be particularly on Danish
soil. Historically, the geotechnical community of Denmark has classified soils
into organic and inorganic types. Organic soils are made from plant and animal
material and are generally less suitable for foundations. Another way to classify
soils is on the basis of their geological age. Older soils typically exhibit higher
strength due to historical compression of the ice caps. Soils can be broadly cate-
gorized into four types: Pre-quaternary, Glacial, Late Glacial, and Post-Glacial.
The distinction between Pre- and Postglacial soils is particularly relevant for
foundation engineering. Soils consolidated under ice caps during the last ice age
(Pre-Glacial) generally offer better foundation properties compared to newer, of-
ten organic rich post-glacial soils (Oversen et al., 2017). These classifications are
6
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
needed to evaluate the Geotechnical Categories introduced in 4.2.1. The eval-
uation of the type of soil should be based on experience (Standard, 2007d) or
various geotechnical tests. This evaluation is based on grain size, color, pattern
and sorting (Oversen et al., 2017). Additionally, soil that primarily contains sand
is defined as friction soil. If it consists mainly of clay, it is classified as a cohesion
soil. Properties such as the undrained shear strength of the soil, the angle of fric-
tion, and the unit weight are essential parameters. Since 1888, Danish soil types
have been systematically mapped using a grid-based survey method. Geus has
conducted a complete evaluation of the Danish underground, see Figure 3.1a,
to give a better overview of the soils that can be expected in any given area
(Soltau, 2023). For one-family houses, characterizing the type of soil, comple-
mented by strength measurements, is generally sufficient to assess the suitability
of any given solution. However, larger and more complex structures often require
a more advanced and detailed soil analysis. This differentiation emphasizes the
varying levels of complexity and requirements in foundation engineering based
on the scale of the structure (Oversen et al., 2017).
In practice, cohesion soils have cohesive strength, but the angle of friction is
evaluated to be 0. In addition, they are more susceptible to settlements. Friction
soils are evaluated to have no cohesive strengths and instead obtain strength
through its angle of friction and the stress imposed by the soil. For any direct
foundation, the depth at which it is placed will increase the capacity as the soil
stress increases based on the total density of the soil. This stress is reduced by
the presence of water, and a distinction is made between total and effective stress
(Oversen et al., 2017). To get an overview of the different soil properties that will
be used for application in Chapter 6, they are shown in table 3.1. These values
are general estimations based on the type of soil (StructX, 2023) (Geotechdata,
2023).
Table 3.1: Soil Properties
Description

3.1.1
Soil Testing
Soil tests help inform the properties of the soil. The different tests most com-
monly used in geotechnical sites for smaller structures are shown in Table 3.2
(Budhu, 2011).
Understanding soil conditions makes design choices more in-
formed and reduces the risk of structural damage, as well as increasing the pos-
sibility of a more optimized design. The necessary amount of soil evaluation is
based on the structure (Standard, 2007d).

Description
Visual Inspection
Preliminary step involving observation of the
soil’s surface, vegetation, and visible signs of wa-
ter presence. This also includes taking into ac-
count neighboring structures.
Test Pits
Digging pits for direct soil examination, useful
to obtain soil samples.
Shear vane test
Soil’s vane strength is determined, which is nor-
mally an expression of the soil’s undrained shear
strength.

(CPT)
Involves pushing a cone into the soil and mea-
suring resistance, providing continuous soil layer
data.
Standard
Penetration
Test (SPT)
Measures resistance of soil to penetration by a
sampler tube, used to assess soil density and me-
chanical properties.
3.2
Structural Components
The shape and dimensions of the foundation directly affect its bearing capacity.
On the basis of the materials used for the foundation, the structural analysis
changes considerably. These are evaluated according to their respective norm
and will not be described in more detail, but will be addressed when applying
the design methods in Sections 6.2.3 and 6.1.10.
Structural analysis in foundation design also includes an evaluation of the general
stability of the structure (Standard, 2007d). Historically, this topic has received
little attention, mainly because of the most commonly used materials for building
foundations, such as brick or concrete. These are typically connected to bearing
members, such as walls or columns, through mortar or concrete and therefore
ensure stability (Holmberg, 1882). Although traditionally not a primary con-
cern in geotechnical engineering, it cannot be overlooked. Specifically, for the
application of the proposed design method in Chapter 6, the wind load presents
a challenge.
9
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
3.2.1
Foundation Types
Having a complete understanding of various solutions leads to better decision-
making, as the design can be customized to meet the unique requirements of a
specific situation. Several different types of foundation are introduced to give
a broader understanding of the variety of solutions. These are found in Table
3.3.
Solutions are divided into categories of solutions for direct foundations
and pile foundations. In addition, ground improvement methods are mentioned
(København, 1960).
Table 3.3: Foundation solutions
Direct foundations
Pile Foundation
Ground
Improve-
ment
Point foundation
Caissons
Chemical injection
Strip foundation
Drilled piles
Electromosse
Combined foundations
Driven piles
Compaction of soil lay-
ers (rolling)
Plate foundation
Compaction of soil lay-
ers (vibration)
Deep drainage
Most commonly used solutions are strip, point and plate foundations. For cases
where stronger soils lie too deep for direct foundations, piloting is commonly
used (Pedersen, 2011). Ground Improvement is mainly used in combination with
direct foundations to increase capacities. They are rarely the only application
used to carry a structure (København, 1960). Moving forward, the focus will be
on strip foundations and piles, as these are used for application in Chapter 6.
Their soil bearing capacities are explained in Sections 3.3 and 3.4.
Strip foundations, often referred to as continuous footings, are horizontal struc-
tures that run along the length of a building. They are best suited to support the
load bearing walls of structures such as one-family houses. The strip foundations
evenly distribute the structural load. This makes them ideal for structures with
uniformly distributed loads. Their robustness ensures stability and is a preferred
choice for residential buildings and low-rise structures (Jacobsen, 1992)
For pile foundations, different materials can be used; most commonly used is
reinforced concrete, steel, and for some particular structures, wood (Pedersen,
2011). A specific type of steel pile is called screw piles. These can be easily
10
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
installed and uninstalled without damaging the pile itself or nearby structures.
Their ease of installation significantly increases their reuseability. This intro-
duces the possibility of reducing the carbon footprint of the foundation when a
recycled material is used. They consist of helical screw-shaped elements that are
mechanically installed in the ground. Screw foundations are particularly advan-
tageous for foundations in remote or environmentally sensitive areas. A downside
of working with piles is that the estimation of their capacity can become tedious.
This is because, in contrast to direct foundations, they can have their structural
properties altered during installation (Jens Jakob Porsmose, 2023)
3.3
Direct Foundations - Soil Capacity
Soil bearing capacity refers to the maximum load that the soil can safely carry
without undergoing excessive settlement or shear failure (Jacobsen, 1992). This
thesis will not involve calculations of eccentric-loaded foundations and will focus
on the vertical bearing capacities of the foundation. Additionally, where param-
eters can be chosen, if uncertainties make a precise estimation difficult, they are
chosen with safety precautions. This ensures that the safety of the structure is
maintained and is in accordance with the norm (Standard, 2007d).
Focusing on direct foundations, Karl Terzaghi invented a theory for the bearing
capacity of soil based on plasticity theory. The bearing capacity formula consists
of three contributions, one from the surcharge q, one from the cohesion c, and
one from the density of the soil (Oversen et al., 2017).
R = 1

undrained case, which typically will investigate friction soil types and cohesion
soil types, respectively. The overall equation can be further elaborated as per
3.2. An explanation of the different parts of the equation is elaborated in the
following tables.

When looking at Equation 3.2 specifically for friction soils, the main difference

(Oversen et al., 2017) and can be found as tabulated values(Pedersen, 2011). For
friction, the most critical case is the drained case. This results in a simplification
of Equation 3.2. As the sand does not have a cohesive strength and depth factors
are neglected, the formula is defined according to Equation 3.4.

3.4
Piloting - Soil Capacity
When designing a pile it can be done in multiple ways. These methods include
static loading trials, dynamic loading trials, and geostatic calculation. The first
two mentions include measurements of the pile and soil before, during, and after
the pile is placed. This is because altering the soil will change the parameters
that affect the bearing capacity (Standard, 2007d). These measurements cannot
be done in this project and the geostatic calculation will be used. Geostatic
formulas are based on the type of soil, which has led to the creation of empirical
formulas. These formulas differ from country to country (Oversen et al., 2017).
The bearing capacity comes from two contributions, as seen in Equation 3.5. The
contributions come from the surface area of the pile Rs and the tip of the pile Rb
(Standard, 2007d). These contributions are affected by whether the body or tip
of the pile is placed in friction or cohesion soil. Therefore, the bearing capacity
is based on the combination of these evaluations. Their respective contributions
are found in the following sections.
It should be noted that when the tip is
embedded in sand additional measurements in the form of a test must be made
and the bearing capacity should not entirely rely on geotechnical calculation.
Additionally, it is always preferred to have measurement of the pile (Standard,
2007d).
Rt = Rb + Rs
(3.5)

3.5
Serviceability Limit State
The characteristic load will induce stress on the soil during the lifetime of the
structure. This load will continually apply pressure on the soil, making it con-
tract, leading to settlements. The response of the soil to structural loads can be
categorized into three types of settlement explained in Table 3.12. Settlements
are one of the primary causes of structural damage (Pedersen, 2011). Unfortu-
15
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
nately, it is also one of the areas that bears the most uncertainty. It has not
yet been possible to precisely describe these deformations because they are influ-
enced by many different parameters that have proven difficult to describe within
a certain set of equations. These parameters include grain size, grain distribu-
tion, drainage possibilities, and the geological history of loading (Oversen et al.,
2017). Notably, for direct foundations the Geotechnical Categories that will be
introduced in section 4.2.1 allow not to evaluate settlements in categories 1 and
2 (Pedersen, 2011),
Table 3.12: Types of Settlements and Their Characteristics
Type of Settlement
Description
Initial
Occurs immediately as a new load is applied to the soil.
Consolidation
Significantly influenced by soil type. In clay soils, this
type of settlement is more pronounced due to the longer
drainage time, leading to structural settlements as wa-
ter is expelled from the soil.
Creep
Generally has the least impact, depending on whether
the soil is normally consolidated. Previously loaded soil
tends to exhibit less creep settlement.
Settlements caused by sand layers in mixed clay-sand soils are typically minor
enough to be negligible in practice. On the contrary, fat clay soils can expe-
rience substantial settlements over time, leading to issues such as differential
settlements. With this in mind, information on the types of soil present and the
evaluation of settlements can be done based on experience to better inform a
design choice (Bowles, 1997).
When investigating piles for their settlements, specifically for one-family houses,
it is usually enough to identify the negative surface tension on the specific pile
(Pedersen, 2011). Surface tension is found as one of the following values.
1. The load that will initiate settlements within the volume, which is defined
by a 1/2 slope vertically. In 3D this will be a cone shape. If multiple piles
are placed in a near vicinity, the load is reduced.
2. The geostatical surface tension calculated above the bearing capacity layer
(Standard, 2007d).
16
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
3.6
Additional Considerations
To ensure good practice, some additional considerations must be applied when
choosing the foundation design. These areas have been identified by previously
occurring errors. First, the foundation should obtain a frost-free depth, which is
described as 0.9-1.2 meters below the surface (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2018). It
is possible to not adhere to these values if, for example, insulation is introduced.
(Standard, 2007d).
Another critical consideration for a foundation design is the level of groundwa-
ter and any water present around the foundation. This specific observation is
highlighted because the most common problems are often due to water around
the structure. If the groundwater level increases, the structure can be subject to
the impact of these forces and can cause serious structural damage (Pedersen,
2011).
Geotechnical solutions typically draw from proven methods and local experience,
meaning that there often isn’t an absolute right or wrong approach, provided that
the structural integrity is preserved over the structure’s lifespan.
Safety factors in geotechnical engineering are a critical aspect that directly influ-
ence the structural integrity and longevity of the construction. In particular, the
safety factors are typically higher compared to other areas of structural engineer-
ing (Standard, 2007d). This level of caution is a sign of some clear uncertainties
in determining the properties within the soil even with the knowledge that is in
the area. The norm currently does not allow for a decrease in the safety factors
if more well-defined properties can be established. This gives little incentive to
create a more in-depth analysis.
3.7
Partial Conclusion
The properties of soil and how they are found have now been introduced, as
well as the most widely applied foundations in the Danish context. Additionally,
the bearing calculation for direct foundations and piles has been introduced. In
summary, the calculation of the bearing capacity for strip foundations follows
the Terzaghi Equation 3.2. The core equations for direct foundations are for the
cohesion soil case in Equation 3.3 and for the friction soil case found in Equation
3.4. The equations presented in this chapter undergo modifications when applied
17
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3. Foundation Design Principles - Literature Review
to the case, but will yield the same result given the criteria set by the project.
This includes not investigating eccentrically loaded foundations, the geometry of
the foundation, and in practice for cohesion soil the angle of friction is evaluated
as 0 and for friction soil the cohesion is 0 (Pedersen, 2011). Furthermore, this
project will not consider horizontal loading on the foundations. For piles, the
four equations 3.6, 3.7, 3.8, 3.9 are used accordingly.
In Chapter 5 a closer
analysis of the parameters that influence the calculation of the capacity of direct
and pile foundations is carried out.
Before the calculations can be applied, it is necessary to outline the two design
methods, both the existing and proposed: this is done in the following chapter.
18
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 4. Introduction of Design Methods
4
Introduction of Design Methods
While the sector of foundation design for one-family houses may not account
for the majority of emissions in the general construction industry, it highlights
critical aspects of foundation design.
This chapter outlines the existing and proposed design method, focusing on cur-
rent practices in foundation design in Denmark. The existing design method is
defined in part by talking to practitioners(Jens Jacob Porsmose, 2024), (Vester-
gaard, 2023) and based on the guidance literature(Pedersen, 2011). The core
of the existing design method is the Geotechnical Categories which are further
elaborated in section 4.2.1. The existing design method is directly outlined in
Figure 4.2. By further developing the existing design method, a proposed design
method is introduced. The proposed design method is an integrated approach
that involves multiple design considerations. Recognizing the complexity of the
soil, the method admits the challenge of generalizing any single approach. How-
ever, the development of a generalized solution remains essential for challenging
current practices. Although it may not offer a universal remedy for all scenarios,
it stimulates important discussions about foundation practices. The proposed
design method is described both as an overall approach, see Figure 4.5, and in
more detail as a step-by-step guide, see Figure 4.6.
4.1
Historic Point of View
The discipline of geotechnical engineering is relatively young, particularly in
Denmark. It was not until the beginning of the nineteenth century that engineers
began collecting empirical data for practical use (København, 1960). Looking
back at solutions used throughout Danish history gives a broader picture of the
existing design method and how the approach to making foundations on Danish
soil has evolved.
It clearly shows that the area has become more and more
19
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 4. Introduction of Design Methods
informed about the properties and solutions of the soil and points to further
optimization of this in the future (København, 1960). Additionally, this thesis
does not want to waste time solving a problem that already has been solved. Iron
screws have been known as foundation methods since the late 1800s, but have
rarely been used up until recent years (Thortsen, 1890). This type of foundation
has gained in popularity in recent years in the form of screw foundations (Jens
Jakob Porsmose, 2023). This is a great example of using previously acquired
knowledge, and one of the reasons why the solution is not applied more broadly
is limitations imposed by the norm. (Jens Jakob Porsmose, 2023). Looking at
the previous norm (Standard, 1998) and guidance literature (Larsen, 1994) it
can help evaluate how the existing design method has evolved in recent years.
4.2
Existing Design Method
The definition of the existing design method of foundation construction is based
on the literature on the topic; this includes the guidance literature, as well as the
norm (Pedersen, 2011) (Standard, 2007d). The literature only partially describes
the existing design method. To gain a better understanding of how it is applied in
practice and to anchor it in the actual geotechnical society of Denmark, the topic
was discussed with geotechnical engineers, Jens Porsmose (Jens Jacob Porsmose,
2024) and Nicolaj Vestergaard (Vestergaard, 2023).
With their input, a list
of design steps was formed. This list, as a flowchart, serves as the outline of
the existing design method. The flow chart can be found in Figure 4.1. The
definition is limited by the information obtained during the process and could
be contested by other practitioners, as they might have different approaches,
but in this project it will serve as the outline of the approach. It should be
mentioned that the steps of choosing a Geotechnical Category and choosing
the foundation type are intertwined, and based on the foundation choice, the
Geotechnical Category could change (Jens Jacob Porsmose, 2024). Note that
the first step of the design method is assumed to be information obtained from
other parties and that the design cannot influence this step.
At the core of this method are the Geotechnical Categories. Specifically, the
foundation community in Denmark works with three Geotechnical Categories
(Pedersen, 2011).
The Geotechnical Categories and the method of placing a
design within them are evaluated in the following section.
20
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 4. Introduction of Design Methods
Figure 4.1: Flowchart Guide - The Existing Design Method
4.2.1
Geotechnical Categories
The initial idea behind the Geotechnical Categories goes back to the foundation
classes of the previous SBI and was created to make the design process easier and
more affordable (Larsen, 1994). Each category requires adherence to a different
set of criteria. The engineer has the responsibility to categorize the foundation by
assessing it against these criteria. Certain criteria allow interpretation based on
experience, for example, determining whether the soil is of Glacial/Late Clacial
origin (Pedersen, 2011). To give a better overview of how a design can be placed
within categories, a decision tree method is used; see Figure 4.2. This way of
presenting the method serves to give an understanding of the steps involved and
where changes might be possible (Pedersen, 2011).
Although this approach makes the overall decision making easier, it also restricts
the design. Arguably, the engineer will try to place the structure in as low a
category as possible to reduce costs. Furthermore, it is always allowed to place
a structure in a higher category as this leads to a more thorough evaluation,
but there is currently little incentive, as this leads to increased costs (Pedersen,
2011).
4.2.2
Cost Evaluation
The cost evaluation is not directly part of the design method, but is nonetheless
also a point of interest. Currently, two things are the main focus of a foundation
21
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 4. Introduction of Design Methods
design process. The structural health of the structure throughout its lifetime,
and secondly, the costs of designing and establishing this foundation should be
kept to a minimum (Pedersen, 2011). Figure 4.3 clearly shows a focus primarily
on cost. A proposed change could include environmental costs, such as CO2
emissions, when evaluating the success of a design. This will be further addressed
in Chapter 7.
Figure 4.3: Estimated Price of Foundation Solutions
4.3
Proposed Design Method
The existing design method checks the criteria to simplify the solution. This
results in a backward approach, where the design is limited by the method,
rather than having the design govern the approach. On the basis of the existing
design method, a suggestion for improvement is proposed. It is important to
emphasize that the nature of the proposed design method is not to disregard the
current practice but to expand the method to allow for more freedom in design.
The proposed design method is, in the same way, a guidance tool for the design
of foundations. Looking at the foundation design process, it can be divided into
3 sections, found in Figure 4.4. To include areas that have a significant effect on
the total load applied to the ground, the proposed design method will be better
suited to address various problems. It will be possible to influence the design of
the structure above the foundation based on the soil properties and introduce
the possibility of having the soil governing the design.
22
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 4. Introduction of Design Methods
Figure 4.4: Three Areas of Foundation Design
These three areas depend on each other, each with their own list of decisions and
evaluations. Based on the information obtained in one area, informed decisions
can be made in the other categories. The method is inspired by the existing
method and an overall approach when it comes to the design of structural ele-
ments (Shaopei Lin, 2015). An elaboration of the considerations found in each
of the sections is found in Table 4.1.

limit the design possibilities and will depend on the choices made in the previous

4.4
Evaluation of Design Methods
A set of criteria is described to evaluate the two design methods. This is done
to increase comparability and ensure that the evaluation is focused. The specific
criteria are found in Table 4.4. They are given a score of 1-5 which is further
elaborated in Table 4.3 and are chosen based on what the thesis wants to eval-
uate in the design methods. It focuses on parameters that are important for
implementation in practice and those (Guillaume Marques, 2010).
4.5
Partial Conclusion
Geotechnical Categories function as a design tool for engineers in Denmark, help-
ing to differentiate projects. Its established presence in the industry standardizes

collaboration between companies and the integration of new team members into
the process. However, it involves some simplification and requires meeting cer-
tain criteria.
By reorganizing and expanding the existing design method, the proposed design
method offers a more comprehensive view of the foundation design. Dividing the
design into three distinct but interrelated sections – load evaluation, soil analy-
sis, and structural analysis. This approach not only accounts for the technical
aspects but also incorporates considerations for environmental sustainability and
innovation. The proposed design method challenges conventional practices by
shifting the focus from criteria-based solutions to more adaptable and integrated
evaluations.
The performance criteria introduced serve as the evaluation basis of the ap-
plication of the two design methods and are crucial to evaluating the success.
The scoring system allows for easy comparison and highlights the strengths and
weaknesses of both methods. By now both methods have been introduced and
it is time to evaluate the formulas used for capacity calculation to get a better
understanding of which parameters have the biggest influence on the design.

Chapter 5. Analysis of Calculation Methods
5
Analysis of Calculation Methods
A sensitivity analysis of the bearing capacity formulas is now introduced. This
technique is used to identify and understand the impact of various critical param-
eters on foundation performance. Once these critical parameters are identified,
they are individually modified to assess their effects on bearing capacity perfor-
mance (Borgonovo, 2023). Observations are made on how changes in each pa-
rameter distinctly influence the foundation’s capacity. This approach highlights
the individual impact of each variable on the overall capacity of the foundation.
The evaluation is done on both direct foundations in Section 5.1.1 and piles in
Section 5.1.2.
This approach is particularly relevant when critically reviewing calculation meth-
ods. For the equations in question that are arguably simple, it might seem re-
dundant, but visualizing the capacities still brings insight regardless. Sensitivity
analysis not only aids in optimizing foundation design choices but also plays a
key role in risk assessment. Understanding which parameters significantly affect
foundation capacity is crucial for identifying potential failure modes and devel-
oping strategies to mitigate these risks (Saltelli et al., 2004). For discussion of
the impact of these parameters, they can be split into two categories. Those
dependant on design choices, width, depth, and choice of foundation type. And
those dependent on the soil in question, the cohesive strength, angle of friction,
and unit weight of the soil.
Furthermore, specifically the table introduced for direct foundations in Geotech-
nical Category 1 is evaluated; see Figure 5.5. Bearing capacity formulas are used
to evaluate how these tabulated values are used, which might serve to inform
the proposed design method to create similar solutions for future investigations.
This is found in Section 5.2
30
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 5. Analysis of Calculation Methods
5.1
Sensitivity Analysis
The sensitivity analysis is performed by defining the minimum and maximum
values for the parameters included in the bearing capacity formulas. The val-
ues used for the specific parameters of the soil are based on the knowledge of
the soil described in Table 3.1. For parameters that depend on design choices,
an estimate is made of probable values based on information on materials and
common-occurring dimensions. A complete overview of the parameters and their
initial and maximum values can be found in Table 5.1. Each range is divided
into exactly 100 steps. For each parameter, the remaining parameters remain
at their initial value and the parameter in question is increased according to its
range. The resulting graphs should be evaluated on the basis of these sets of
parameters, as different initial and maximum values will yield different results.
The calculations used for the sensitivity analysis are found in Appendix H.

The analysis suggests that the soil properties in either case have the greatest

the governing parameter, and in the friction case the stresses in the soil and the
bearing capacity factor Nq are most significant.

5.1.2
Piloting
The pile capacity formulas are directly evaluated using previously introduced
equations: 3.6, 3.7, 3.8 and 3.9.
As both parameters in the friction surface
equation depend on depth, the contribution is evaluated on the depth, unit
weight of the soil, and the circumference of the pile. For piles in cohesion soils,
32
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 5. Analysis of Calculation Methods
Figure 5.2: Direct Friction - In opposition to the cohesion case, the overburden
pressure is the most dominant followed by the bearing capacity factor Nq, both
dependant on soil properties
it is again found that the undrained shear strength has the greatest influence on
the capacity. For friction piles, structural concerns have a higher impact, and it
is highly dependent on the depth of the pile. Additionally, the addition of tip
resistance is significantly higher for friction piles compared to cohesion piles.
5.1.3
Evaluation on Sensitivity Analysis
Although the evaluations are done separately for each soil type, it shows that
based on soil conditions, different design choices should be made. In this case, for
example, an increase in the width of a direct foundation on friction soil provides
a greater increase in capacity than would be the case for cohesion soil. And for
piles, the diameter of the piles will have a significantly smaller impact on cohesion
soils compared to friction soils. This evaluation highlights the importance of
understanding the properties of the soil to better inform design choices.
5.2
Tabulated Values of Geotechnical Category 1
The Geotechnical Categories introduce a design framework. In certain cases, it
may serve more as an impediment than an aid, as it potentially does not en-
courage a thorough exploration of the foundation design (Jens Jacob Porsmose,
33
==PAGE:43==

(b) Piles Cohesion - Tip

undrained shear strength of the soil has the highest contribution to the bearing
capacity. Piles with their tip in the cohesion soil get a small contribution from
this part.
2024). An evaluation of a direct foundation is carried out in Geotechnical Cate-
gory 1 to obtain more detailed information on the values presented in Figure 5.5
(Pedersen, 2011). Since Geotechnical Categories 2 and 3 use identical bearing
capacity formulas, these will be implemented using the specific set of parameters
required for the application of this; Table 5.5. The category describes a mini-

m2 . A summary of the criteria can be found in

introduced values found in Table 3.1.

==PAGE:44==
Chapter 5. Analysis of Calculation Methods
Figure 5.5: Table 1 from Geotechnical Category 1
Based on the results of Table 5.2, a load of 50 kN/m would not introduce a risk of
failure. With estimated properties and a depth of 0.9 meters to obtain a frost-free
depth, the results show that the tabulated values can ensure a safe design when
evaluated against bearing capacity formulas. For calculations, see Appendix A.
This indicates that incorporating such tabulated values into the proposed design
methodology may be possible. Introducing such tables for different materials or
foundation compositions could help introduce innovative solutions.
5.3
Partial Conclusion
In the examination of direct foundations for cohesive soils, shear strength pre-
dominantly influences the design, while for friction soils, both overburden pres-
sure and bearing capacity factor Nq contribute significantly, as detailed in Sec-
tion 5.1.1. Through the analysis, it is observed that for cohesion piles, the shear
strength of the soil emerges as the dominant factor. For friction piles, the di-
ameter plays a more pivotal role due to its contribution to the tip resistance
compared to the cohesion pile. The biggest contributor to friction piles is the
depth of the soil layer to which it is subjected.
These insights highlight the variable impact of different factors on pile perfor-
mance, depending on their interaction with soil characteristics. The most promi-
nent factors are based on soil characteristics for cohesion soils, and for friction
soils, a greater advantage comes from structural concerns such as the dimensions
of the pile. In general, this puts emphasis on the geotechnical test done on each
site.
35
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 5. Analysis of Calculation Methods
The exploration of various parameters governing foundation design has revealed
the complex nature of soil, underscoring the challenge of applying a design
method that includes all cases. This complexity reduces the practicality of a
universal solution.
Consequently, the importance of tailoring the foundation
design to accommodate specific soil characteristics is further emphasized.
Analysis of Geotechnical Category 1 revealed that the tabulated values ensure a
safe design, and this opens a potential avenue for the introduction of innovative
materials. If tables like this are created for less emission heavy materials it could
further their implementation.
Having analyzed the bearing capacity formulas, the focus now shifts to assessing
other aspects of the design methods. This evaluation will be conducted through
their application to Tversted House. This approach will produce two designs
for comparison, allowing the strengths and weaknesses of each design method
to emerge during the application process. This application follows in the next
chapter.

Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods
6
Application of Design Methods
The two design methods introduced in Chapter 4 will now be applied to the
case study, and both design a solution that is adequately suited to the soil
conditions found beneath Tversted House. This chapter will begin by applying
the proposed design method in Section 6.1, followed by application of the existing
design method outlined in Section 6.2. The use of the case study will highlight
the different designs that the methods allow and help identify areas in which
each design method might be superior to the other. It can also help identify
critical areas in the design process that may have been overlooked until now.
The solutions that this application of the design methods yield should not be
seen as final design.
6.1
Application of Proposed Design Method
The proposed design method will now be applied to the case study. It is impor-
tant to note that even though the step-by-step guide, as described in Figure 4.6,
is applied in chronological order, it is possible to go back and forth and change
parameters to increase the viability of design. The design is generally inspired
by the solution found at Tversted House, but is not identical.
6.1.1
Structural Design
Four different FEM-models of the structure are constructed in the software Robot
Structural Analysis 2023. Robot Structural Analysis Professional is a structural
analysis software developed by Autodesk. It is used to simulate and analyze var-
ious structural designs. The accuracy of any FEM model depends on the quality
of the input data, and the exact design of the Tversted House was not available.
Therefore, this design cannot be directly evaluated against the actual design. As
this project focuses on the design of the foundation, the structural design of the
39
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods
FEM-model is not assessed in terms of viability. This will introduce uncertain-
ties. Even with these insecurities, it was evaluated to be informative to construct
the models, and the design results will be evaluated based on these limitations.
The models differ in their structural components and support conditions to high-
light the difference that structural design choices can have. The models include
the timber beams of the foundation but excludes the cellglas blocks. The full
description of geometry, releases, support conditions, list of members, and loads
for each model can be found in Appendix B.
It is expected that structural changes to the models will show that adjustments
lead to a significant change in the transferred load on the soil. The structural
changes include adding wind crosses to the structure, adding additional beams,
and changing the support conditions in two different ways. Further elaboration
on the specific changes is found in Section 6.1.7. The loads applied to the struc-
ture are found in Section 6.1.2 with the resulting reaction forces found in Section
6.1.8.
Figure 6.1: Tversted House - Actual Design at Tversted House
Figure 6.2: RSA Model of Tversted House

6.1.2
Load Calculations
Load calculations are according to current norms (Standard, 2007a), (Standard,
2007b) and (Standard, 2007c). Wind load and snow load are based on the form of
the structure and the wind load is also impacted on where the structure is placed.
The estimation of the snow load does not take into account the accumulation of
snow.
The structure is located near the west coast of Jutland and, based on the sur-
rounding landscape, is placed in terrain category 1. This information results in
an average wind speed of 27 m/s. To reduce the complexity of wind load esti-
mation, the structure is seen as two mono-pitch buildings named Section 1 and
Section 2.
The simplifications do not have an effect on the direct comparison of the design
methods, as the loads and resulting reactions used to evaluate the two design
methods will be identical. The load values are found in Tables 6.1, 6.2, and 6.3.
A full calculation is found in Appendix C.

6.1.5
Design Concerns
When comparing the two borehole results, a substantial difference in soil strength
is found. The soil types seem to be more or less the same, but the difference in
the tests raises a concern as to how the conditions might be elsewhere on site.
Uncertainties such as this make it necessary to have high partial safety factors in
geotehcnical designs. This could be counteracted by additional holes to obtain a
more in-depth understanding of soil conditions. There is little incentive, however,
as the norm does not allow for a reduction of safety factors based on additional
information (Standard, 2007d). The current soil composition introduces the risk
of permeation and will have to be addressed in the foundation design (Oversen
et al., 2017).
6.1.6
Foundation Choices
The foundation is created as a strip foundation using a combination of glulam
beams and cellglas and the build-up is found in Figure 6.4a. This build-up is
inspired by the solution found on-site. The structural considerations that this
introduces are based on information from the actual design (Martiny, 2023),
44
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods
(Casablanca, 2023), (Aarsø, 2023).
(a) Proposed Design
(b) On-Site design
Figure 6.4: Glapor/Timber Build-Up of The Direct Foundation Solution
Cellglas is a relatively old product that at least dates back 100 years. Previously,
the most used case for this material has been in combination with concrete to
create foundations or as a granulate used for roads and roofs. The properties
of the material include having significant compression strength while being light
itself. The material is great for insulation, serves as a vapor barrier, and is non-
flammable. For foundation purposes outside of structural considerations, it also
serves as radon protection (Aarsø, 2023). The reason Glapor was used for this
specific project is because it is made entirely of recycled glass and, therefore,
more sustainable than many competitors on the market (AB, 2023). The timber
member ensures that the load applied to the cellglas is evenly distributed, as the
material is susceptible to point loads. The cellglas blocks are placed on a com-
pressed sand layer to ensure that all surfaces between the blocks are even. This
also ensures that the connection between the blocks and the beams is smooth
and reduces the risk of materials sliding in relation to each other (Martiny, 2023).

6.1.9
Soil Bearing Capacities
The bearing capacity of the soil is found based on calculations of the direct
foundation. The reactions are calculated as the pr-meter of the foundation. The
capacity check is performed for both the sand layer and the silt clay layer to
ensure that there is no permeation. The assumed stress dispersion through the
top layer is assumed to be 1/4 (Oversen et al., 2017). This will increase the
width at which the stress can be obtained for the cohesion layer. The cohesion
layer surcharge does not consider overlaying soils and the summary of the results
is found in Tables 6.10 and 6.11. The soil bearing capacity in both cases is found
to be high enough to withstand the load. With the chosen depth, the foundation
would need insulation. Calculations are found in Appendix A.

glulam beam is checked in bending and shear, as well as in combined bending
and axial force (Casablanca, 2023) (Standard, 2007d). Relevant stresses are ex-

6.1.11
Special Considerations
Based on the most common structural damage to one-family houses, additional
consideration is made for the design.
These were introduced in Section 4.3.
For this case, settlements are an important aspect. To reduce settlements, a
compensated foundation is used. Compensated foundations take it offspring in
the idea that the soil before being subject to the load of a structure is already
subject to some amount of load. This force is equal to the weight of the soil itself.
Under the 0,5-meter-wide foundation, the soil has a density of approximately 18
kN/m3. Removing an area of 0.78 x 0.5 would result in a 7 kN/m reduction of
the load on the underlying soil. No direct evaluation of how this is applied in the
case has been evaluated. The theory on the subject does, however, not take into
account that the soil will possibly rise as the load is removed. This has to do
with the properties of the soil in question and should be evaluated from project
to project. (Jacobsen, 1992). It could be counteracted by placing a dummy load
as the soil is removed. When a light foundation is used in addition to a light
structure, the amount of compensated soil is smaller, and the solution can sustain
the load on the structure. Furthermore, it should be noted that compensated
foundations are usually used when designing a reinforced plate foundation, since
51
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods

the stiffness of the structural material increases sufficiently to ensure an even load
transfer throughout the area (Jacobsen, 1992). In this case, the compensation is
made locally beneath the bearing members of the structure (Aarsø, 2023). Until
now, no settlements have occurred in the actual Tversted House case (Martiny,
2023).
Secondly, the overall stability of the structure needs to be evaluated. For this
lightweight structure, the uplift from the wind is especially problematic. Addi-
tionally, it does not have a heavy foundation that could serve as an anchor. To
solve this problem, tension piles are placed in the ground, and soil conditions in
this regard help due to their negative surface tension induced by the silty clay
layer (Oversen et al., 2017). When evaluating the negative reaction on the pile,
the lesser of the two values is used between the surface tension and the conical
area around the cone.
In this case, it is the surface tension around the pile
(Standard, 2007d). The results of the tension pile are found in Table 6.15. It
shows that a single pile will not be able to introduce the necessary safety. The
placement of enough piles to withstand local uplift will have to be evaluated in
a final design. By reviewing the reaction forces in different load combinations,
the overall structural validity is assessed. It is estimated that a total number of
10 piles is needed. An evaluation of this approach is given in Chapter 8.
Finally, the evaluation of the remaining criteria introduced in Section 4.2 has

6.2
Application of Existing Design Method
The existing design method will be applied according to the flow chart explained
in Figure 4.1. Information about the structure has already been obtained and
covered, as well as geotechnical investigations. No geotechnical report is con-
structed. The next step is to choose a geotechnical category.
6.2.1
Choosing Geotechnical Category
Using the decision trees in Figure 4.2 helps to place the project in a Geotechnical
Category.
The load is less than 100 kN/m, there is no risk to neighboring
structures, and the excavation is not below groundwater levels. According to the
technical test, the soil strength is too low to place in Geotechnical Category 1,
and none of the criteria for placing smaller structures in Geotechnical Category
3 are found. Therefore, Geotechnical Category 2 is chosen.

6.2.2
Foundation Type
Piles are chosen as the foundation solution as they are expected to perform
better in terms of an environmental assessment. Usually, when implementing
pile foundations, they are tested in various ways during installation. For more
common practices, such as rammed piles, a journal is kept and helps identify the
capacity of the piles. For screw foundations, a similar approach is applied, and
information about the pile is kept in a torsion journal. This is specifically done
to ensure that the piles are not damaged in a way that makes them unsuitable
for bearing purposes (Jens Jakob Porsmose, 2023). Without measurements of
the pile, the results of the design should be assessed with caution.
The choice of using piles introduces the problem of missing information as the
norm describes that for piles evaluated in Geotechnical Category 2, soil tests
should inform of the area beneath the tip of the pile (Standard, 2007d). Con-
sequently, the proposed design will depend on confirming that the soil possesses
certain essential characteristics. To allow the use of only the geostatical formu-
54
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods
las according to 4.2.1 (Pedersen, 2011), it is assumed that the soil is a type of
cohesion. These assumptions will be discussed in more detail in Chapter 8. This
will not change the chosen Geotechnical Category.
Geotechnical Category 2 calls for an evaluation of the negative surface tension
imposed on the piles. The piles are evaluated for both ULS and SLS, and if
required resistance is obtained, the settlements of the piles are deemed negligible
(Pedersen, 2011). The negative surface tension will in this case come from the
soil layers above the carrying soil layer. The results are found in Table 6.16 and
full calculations are found in Appendix A.

6.2.3
Structural Analysis
With a relatively large difference in the diameter and length of the pile, in
combination with current soil layers, there is a risk that the piles buckle under
the load of the structure (Standard, 2007d). The analysis is done to check if the
structural member capacity governs the design, see Table 6.17. The calculation
finds that the buckling will govern the design and do not have the required
strength to obtain the maximum load. The load can, however, be reduced if the
structure obtains a certain stiffness.

6.2.4
Partial Conclusion
The proposed design method introduces the use of non-traditional foundation
materials like cellglas and timber. This approach is tailored to the unique chal-
56
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 6. Application of Design Methods
lenges of Tversted House. The method employs the concept of a compensated
foundation, ensuring equilibrium between pre-existing and new loads to address
potential settlement issues. Furthermore, it incorporates anchors to ensure sta-
bility against uplift. It shows that changes in the design of the building will
impact the resulting loads on the ground. And by including these steps in the
foundation design, additional design freedom is introduced. This approach to the
design of the foundations introduces the possibility of making the foundations
govern the design of the building. The additional steps will, however, increase
the need for architects, structural engineers, and geotechnical engineers to work
more closely together to produce a more optimized design.
In contrast, the existing design method employs Geotechnical Categories. This
approach categorizes the project into Geotechnical Category 2 due to soil condi-
tions and opts for pile foundations as a practical solution. When these specific
soil conditions are found, they often result in a pile foundation according to
Geotechnical Categories (Pedersen, 2011). The method is easy to apply and has
shown to work well, but does limit the design.
In the application of the design methods, a considerable amount of assumptions
were made. Specifically, for reactions and for soil test when designing pile foun-
dations. This makes a precise evaluation of the solutions difficult and will further
influence the evaluation of the material usage. This impact will be discussed in
more detail in Chapter 8. Even though the design methods are limited by this
knowledge, it clearly shows that the lack of information on a specific project
limits design significantly. It also leads to a less precise design and to unwanted
consumption of materials.
In conclusion, this chapter highlights the contrast between the proposed de-
sign method and the existing design method. Although the proposed method
demonstrates adaptability and the use of more sustainable materials suited to
specific site conditions, the existing design method underscores the reliability
and robustness of established engineering practices. It becomes clear that the
proposed design method while allowing for innovative solutions also demands
engineers who are familiar with these solutions, materials, etc. The argument
then becomes whether or not the freedom of choice increases viability or if the
existing design method is still superior. To easier conclude on this, both design
methods are evaluated on previously introduced performance criteria found in
Table 4.4. This evaluation follows in the next chapter.
57
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 7. Evaluation of Design Methods
7
Evaluation of Design Methods
This chapter will evaluate each design method based on the performance criteria
introduced previously. In addition, the material usage and the costs it induces
will be added to the evaluation in terms of both economic and environmental
costs. The estimation of these costs is found in Section 7.1. To ensure that
the evaluation of the design methods is grounded in practice, an interview with
Jens Porsmose (Jens Jacob Porsmose, 2024) was conducted.
This evaluation
discusses how the design methods are defined in Chapter 4 and their application
in Chapter 6.
The performance criteria scoring found in Table 7.6 will then
serve as a guide to the advantages and limitations of each design method and
will spark a discussion of these in Chapter 8.
7.1
Costs
The specific use of materials has a great impact on both economic and environ-
mental costs. In addition, it will evaluate the materials in opposition to each
other. The total amount of material used is based on the design and data sheets
of the structural elements. As the designs are not final, the costs for both meth-
ods should be reviewed with caution. The results are found in Tables; 7.1 and
7.2. The complete calculation of both economic and environmental costs is found
in Appendix F.
The evaluation of environmental cost is based on the EPDs of the materials used
and informs about the C02 emission pr m3 or kg of the specific material. These
evaluations are performed on the basis of an LCA evaluation of the material. This
includes several categories, such as production, material, transport, disposal, etc.
(Energistyrelsen, 2015). The environmental impact of the two design methods is
evaluated in two cases. The first case takes recycling of materials into account,
and the second does not. A further elaboration of these values is found in Table

tion requirements separates it from the existing design method. By introducing
both load and structural considerations into the method, it demonstrates the
potential for future improvements. However, there is room for improvement in
terms of user-friendliness. It represents a shift in approach and will require mul-
tiple iterations to achieve better user-friendliness. In addition, the effectiveness
of the design method depends on integrating other parts of the project team,
which may not be feasible for any given project. Regarding time efficiency, the
implementation of the proposed design method introduces additional steps and
therefore increases the time used for design. Additionally, a more comprehensive
analysis is required to fully comprehend and apply solutions that deviate from
conventional methods. Increased design options can lead to a more complex and
time-intensive process. Furthermore, the proposed design method demonstrates
innovation and contributes significantly to the field’s knowledge base, and, given
time, it could increase knowledge on otherwise unknown designs. Although it
has not been directly evaluated within the application, the unrestricted nature
of the proposed design method suggests potential applicability to projects that
fall beyond the boundaries of the current scope.
When applying the existing design method, it includes categorizations based on
the conditions. When certain criteria are not met, specific solutions are ignored.
This limits the engineer in design choices if he wants to make the design on
previously based experience, and, in turn, his ability to make decisions. This
decreases the flexibility of the solution. While the existing method is broadly
used in today’s geotechnical society, it puts a lot of emphasis on user-friendliness,
resulting in a more straightforward and accessible process for practitioners. This
also increases its efficiency.

restrict the project in terms of environmental costs, but screw piles introduce a
more green alternative to concrete. In the category of innovation and knowledge
